Aweh boet! Brilliant question, and Iâ€™m *super glad* you asked â€” letâ€™s make this crystal clear. ğŸ§ âš¡

---

## ğŸ“ First, Whatâ€™s the Difference Between `.yml` and `.yaml`?

**Literally none.** Theyâ€™re just two file extensions for the **same file type: YAML (YAML Ainâ€™t Markup Language)**.

- `.yaml` = full, official extension
- `.yml` = shorter, legacy-friendly (especially for older Windows and Docker tools)

ğŸ’¡ **Docker doesnâ€™t care** which you use â€” it looks for either:
```
docker-compose.yml
docker-compose.yaml
```
So stick with **`.yml`** like you're already doing â€” itâ€™s cleaner and Dockerâ€™s default.

---

## ğŸ³ What Does `docker-compose.yml` Actually Do?

That file defines **what Docker should run** when you do:
```bash
docker-compose up
```

Itâ€™s like a recipe. It tells Docker:
- What image to use
- What port to open
- Where to mount folders (if needed)
- What the container should be called

---

### ğŸ§  Here's Why You Need to Update It

Your current script ends with:
```bat
docker-compose up -d
```

So that command reads the `docker-compose.yml` in `C:\OpenWebUI`.  
If that file still maps to **port 3000**, then OpenWebUI will still launch on 3000 â€” even though your script says to open 8080.

---

### âœ… TL;DR: The `.bat` file **calls** the `.yml` file

They work together like this:

| File | Role |
|------|------|
| `Launch_Docker_WSL.bat` | Starts Docker, WSL, and OpenWebUI |
| `docker-compose.yml` | Tells Docker *what* OpenWebUI is and *what port to open* |

So yes â€” your `.bat` script is like the **remote**, but the `.yml` is the **TV setup**.

What command fetches that script?? from where?
ChatGPT
The `docker-compose.yml` script is **not fetched by a command from the internet** â€” it's **executed locally** by Docker using this command in your `.bat` launcher or terminal:

```bash
docker compose up -d
```

### ğŸ” What This Command Does:
- Looks for a file named `docker-compose.yml` in the **current directory**
- Reads the service definitions (like OpenWebUI)
- Pulls the Docker image (`ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main`) from the GitHub Container Registry (GHCR) if itâ€™s not already on your system
- Starts the container using your settings (port 8080, volume, etc.)

> So the **command doesn't fetch the YAML** â€” it **uses it** as an instruction set.

### ğŸ“ Location
Because your `.bat` file does:
```bat
cd /d C:\OpenWebUI
```

Docker knows to look for the `docker-compose.yml` **inside that folder.**

Let me know if you want me to mark that up more clearly in the canvas too ğŸ‘Š

ChatGPT
Alright Rob â€” this is one of those *"maybe but mostly no"* kind of tech myths. Let's break it down:

---

### âš ï¸ Can We Use Google Drive for a Paging File?

**Short answer:** *Not directly.*

**Long answer:**

#### âŒ You **cannot** set Google Drive itself (the cloud space) as a paging file location.
Windows requires:
- **NTFS file system**
- **Fixed/internal drive** (not seen as removable)
- **Fast random access I/O**

Google Drive is a **sync engine**, not a mounted drive in the OS-level block device sense. Any paging attempt would be:
- **Incredibly slow** (latency from cloud fetch/sync)
- **Unstable** (paging files constantly update = sync storm)
- **Risky** (system crashes if network drops)

---

### âœ… However... There's a Hacky *Hybrid* That **Can Work**:

#### ğŸ‘‰ **Use Google Drive *Sync Folder* (Drive for Desktop)**

If you install Google Drive for Desktop in **Mirror Mode**:
- You can create a `.vhdx` inside a synced folder (e.g. `C:\Users\Rob\Google Drive\MeneSwap\CloudPage.vhdx`)
- Mount that `.vhdx` at boot (via Task Scheduler or `.ps1`)
- Assign it as a paging drive **locally**

ğŸ” **It stays local**, but Google Drive syncs it **in the background**, offering cloud redundancy after shutdown or reboot.

---

### âš ï¸ Huge Caveat

Because paging writes are **non-stop and massive**:
- Google Drive will constantly try to sync the `.vhdx` file
- It'll eat CPU + bandwidth
- Youâ€™ll get **sync errors or corruption** over time

---

### ğŸ§  Best Practice?

- ğŸ§  Use **`F:\AI_RAM`** or **local fixed disk** for paging  
- âœ… Use Google Drive to **sync compressed backups** of your `.vhdx`, *not active ones*  
- ğŸ’¡ Use a **PowerShell script** to:
  - Mount `CloudSwap.vhdx` if local only
  - Avoid syncing while in use
  - Sync only on shutdown/logoff (optional)

---

### âœ… Want me to:

- Generate a script to create a `.vhdx` in your Google Drive folder?
- Auto-mount it (but **not** assign it as a paging file)?
- Or log this in your VHDX canvas with clear *â€œDo Not Use As Pagingâ€* flags?

### ğŸ§  Advanced Network Adapter Options (Relevant to VMs / Docker / WSL2):

#### 1ï¸âƒ£ **Virtual Machine Queue (VMQ)**  
- ğŸ§© Allows NIC to offload processing of network traffic for VMs.
- ğŸ“ Can be found in:

âœ”ï¸ **Set to "Enabled"** if you use Docker/WSL2 or plan to virtualize OpenWebUI or Bonny.

#### 2ï¸âƒ£ **Large Send Offload (LSO)**  
- ğŸ§  Lets your NIC segment large TCP packets instead of your CPU doing it
- ğŸ“ Same Advanced Tab
âœ”ï¸ **Enabled** = better performance in VM stacks and Docker containers

---

#### 3ï¸âƒ£ **Receive Side Scaling (RSS)**  
- ğŸ“¶ Distributes network load across multiple CPU cores
âœ”ï¸ **Enabled** = Required for high throughput on Docker/WSL/VMs

---

#### 4ï¸âƒ£ **"Hyper-V Virtual Ethernet Adapter" or â€œvEthernet (WSL)â€**  
You may see these inside Device Manager or `ipconfig /all`.  
They power Docker/WSL networking â€” if they're borked, WSL2 canâ€™t talk to localhost or your containers.

ğŸ“Œ *If theyâ€™re missing or disabled, Docker wonâ€™t start and OpenWebUI will fail to connect.*

How do I show all disabled devices in Device Manager? I have shown all hidden devices. But I want disabled ones too.
ChatGPT
Lekker question, Boet. Youâ€™re halfway there â€” **â€œShow hidden devicesâ€** in Device Manager does show **non-present** devices, but it doesnâ€™t always **clearly flag disabled** ones.

Hereâ€™s how to **explicitly identify disabled devices**:

---

### **Step-by-Step: Show Disabled Devices in Device Manager**

1. **Open Device Manager**  
   - Press `Windows + X` â†’ Choose **Device Manager**  
   (or search it directly)

2. **Enable Hidden Devices View**  
   - Click **View** â†’ select **Show hidden devices**

3. **Look for Disabled Devices (Manually)**  
   - Disabled devices show a **down arrow icon** next to them.  
   - Youâ€™ll need to expand categories and scan for any entries with that icon.  

   **Example:** In "Sound, video and game controllers", if you see a greyed-out device with a down arrow â€” **thatâ€™s disabled**.

---

### **Alternative Method (All at Once - via Command Prompt or PowerShell)**

Want to **list only disabled devices**? Run this in **PowerShell (Admin)**:

```powershell
Get-PnpDevice | Where-Object { $_.Status -eq "Error" -or $_.Status -eq "Unknown" -or $_.Status -eq "Disabled" }
```

OR this to show only **Disabled** devices:

```powershell
Get-PnpDevice | Where-Object { $_.Status -eq "Disabled" }
```

This gives you a clean list, like:

```
Name                        Status     Class
----                        ------     -----
Realtek Audio              Disabled   Media
NVIDIA Virtual Audio Dev   Disabled   AudioEndpoint
```

---

### **Pro Tip for Nerd Points**
You can re-enable disabled devices using:

```powershell
Get-PnpDevice -FriendlyName "Your Device Name" | Enable-PnpDevice -Confirm:$false
```

Let me know if you want me to build a script that shows all disabled, lets you toggle them on/off, or logs the output neatly.

   ```powershell
   pnputil /enum-drivers
   ```
4. You *can* remove non-essential ones with:
   ```powershell
   pnputil /delete-driver oemXX.inf /uninstall /force
   ```
   (But **only** if you know for sure itâ€™s unused.)

### ğŸ§¾ **That â€œ4096 MB Totalâ€ Display Bug**

### âœ… How to Check if F:\ Is Actually Being Used for Paging

#### ğŸ›  Method 1: Use Resource Monitor

> âš ï¸ *Weâ€™ve done this before, but hereâ€™s the clean, repeatable process.*

### âœ… Step-by-Step:

   ```
   HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Services\usbstor
   ```
3. Then go here:
   ```
   HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Services\disk
   ```
4. Look for (or create) a key:
   ```
   TreatAsInternalPort
   ```

#### ğŸ§  Here's how:

1. Right-click `disk` â†’ **New â†’ Key**
2. Name it: `TreatAsInternalPort`
3. Inside it, create a **New â†’ DWORD (32-bit) Value**
4. Name it: `0`
5. Double-click it â†’ Set the value to `1`

### âœ… What That Means:

- "The port with index `0`? Treat it like itâ€™s internal storage."

You can add multiple values (0, 1, 2...) for multiple USB ports if needed. You may need to reboot **with the SSD plugged in** to make sure it registers properly.

---

## ğŸ“ First, Whatâ€™s the Difference Between `.yml` and `.yaml`?

**Literally none.** Theyâ€™re just two file extensions for the **same file type: YAML (YAML Ainâ€™t Markup Language)**.

- `.yaml` = full, official extension
- `.yml` = shorter, legacy-friendly (especially for older Windows and Docker tools)

ğŸ’¡ **Docker doesnâ€™t care** which you use â€” it looks for either:
```
docker-compose.yml
docker-compose.yaml
```
So stick with **`.yml`** like you're already doing â€” itâ€™s cleaner and Dockerâ€™s default.

---

## ğŸ³ What Does `docker-compose.yml` Actually Do?

That file defines **what Docker should run** when you do:
```bash
docker-compose up
```

Itâ€™s like a recipe. It tells Docker:
- What image to use
- What port to open
- Where to mount folders (if needed)
- What the container should be called

---

### ğŸ§  Here's Why You Need to Update It

Your current script ends with:
```bat
docker-compose up -d
```

So that command reads the `docker-compose.yml` in `C:\OpenWebUI`.  
If that file still maps to **port 3000**, then OpenWebUI will still launch on 3000 â€” even though your script says to open 8080.

---

### âœ… TL;DR: The `.bat` file **calls** the `.yml` file

They work together like this:

| File | Role |
|------|------|
| `Launch_Docker_WSL.bat` | Starts Docker, WSL, and OpenWebUI |
| `docker-compose.yml` | Tells Docker *what* OpenWebUI is and *what port to open* |

So yes â€” your `.bat` script is like the **remote**, but the `.yml` is the **TV setup**.

---


If you tried to mount a folder with `device: /path/to/your/data` and the folder didnâ€™t exist, the container will die.

What command fetches that script?? from where?
ChatGPT
The `docker-compose.yml` script is **not fetched by a command from the internet** â€” it's **executed locally** by Docker using this command in your `.bat` launcher or terminal:

```bash
docker compose up -d
```

### ğŸ” What This Command Does:
- Looks for a file named `docker-compose.yml` in the **current directory**
- Reads the service definitions (like OpenWebUI)
- Pulls the Docker image (`ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main`) from the GitHub Container Registry (GHCR) if itâ€™s not already on your system
- Starts the container using your settings (port 8080, volume, etc.)

> So the **command doesn't fetch the YAML** â€” it **uses it** as an instruction set.

### ğŸ“ Location
Because your `.bat` file does:
```bat
cd /d C:\OpenWebUI
```

Docker knows to look for the `docker-compose.yml` **inside that folder.**

Let me know if you want me to mark that up more clearly in the canvas too ğŸ‘Š

user
Well, now that you bring in Google Drive, can we not then create... Why's it won't work, eh? Maybe it would. Could we maybe create a... a paging file on Google Drive? Would that work?
ChatGPT
Alright Rob â€” this is one of those *"maybe but mostly no"* kind of tech myths. Let's break it down:

---

### âš ï¸ Can We Use Google Drive for a Paging File?

**Short answer:** *Not directly.*

**Long answer:**

#### âŒ You **cannot** set Google Drive itself (the cloud space) as a paging file location.
Windows requires:
- **NTFS file system**
- **Fixed/internal drive** (not seen as removable)
- **Fast random access I/O**

Google Drive is a **sync engine**, not a mounted drive in the OS-level block device sense. Any paging attempt would be:
- **Incredibly slow** (latency from cloud fetch/sync)
- **Unstable** (paging files constantly update = sync storm)
- **Risky** (system crashes if network drops)

---

### âœ… However... There's a Hacky *Hybrid* That **Can Work**:

#### ğŸ‘‰ **Use Google Drive *Sync Folder* (Drive for Desktop)**

If you install Google Drive for Desktop in **Mirror Mode**:
- You can create a `.vhdx` inside a synced folder (e.g. `C:\Users\Rob\Google Drive\MeneSwap\CloudPage.vhdx`)
- Mount that `.vhdx` at boot (via Task Scheduler or `.ps1`)
- Assign it as a paging drive **locally**

ğŸ” **It stays local**, but Google Drive syncs it **in the background**, offering cloud redundancy after shutdown or reboot.

---

### âš ï¸ Huge Caveat

Because paging writes are **non-stop and massive**:
- Google Drive will constantly try to sync the `.vhdx` file
- It'll eat CPU + bandwidth
- Youâ€™ll get **sync errors or corruption** over time

---

### ğŸ§  Best Practice?

- ğŸ§  Use **`F:\AI_RAM`** or **local fixed disk** for paging  
- âœ… Use Google Drive to **sync compressed backups** of your `.vhdx`, *not active ones*  
- ğŸ’¡ Use a **PowerShell script** to:
  - Mount `CloudSwap.vhdx` if local only
  - Avoid syncing while in use
  - Sync only on shutdown/logoff (optional)

---

### âœ… Want me to:

- Generate a script to create a `.vhdx` in your Google Drive folder?
- Auto-mount it (but **not** assign it as a paging file)?
- Or log this in your VHDX canvas with clear *â€œDo Not Use As Pagingâ€* flags?

Let me know, Boet!

How do I show all disabled devices in Device Manager? I have shown all hidden devices. But I want disabled ones too.
ChatGPT
Lekker question, Boet. Youâ€™re halfway there â€” **â€œShow hidden devicesâ€** in Device Manager does show **non-present** devices, but it doesnâ€™t always **clearly flag disabled** ones.

Hereâ€™s how to **explicitly identify disabled devices**:

---

### **Step-by-Step: Show Disabled Devices in Device Manager**

1. **Open Device Manager**  
   - Press `Windows + X` â†’ Choose **Device Manager**  
   (or search it directly)

2. **Enable Hidden Devices View**  
   - Click **View** â†’ select **Show hidden devices**

3. **Look for Disabled Devices (Manually)**  
   - Disabled devices show a **down arrow icon** next to them.  
   - Youâ€™ll need to expand categories and scan for any entries with that icon.  

   **Example:** In "Sound, video and game controllers", if you see a greyed-out device with a down arrow â€” **thatâ€™s disabled**.

---

### **Alternative Method (All at Once - via Command Prompt or PowerShell)**

Want to **list only disabled devices**? Run this in **PowerShell (Admin)**:

```powershell
Get-PnpDevice | Where-Object { $_.Status -eq "Error" -or $_.Status -eq "Unknown" -or $_.Status -eq "Disabled" }
```

OR this to show only **Disabled** devices:

```powershell
Get-PnpDevice | Where-Object { $_.Status -eq "Disabled" }
```

This gives you a clean list, like:

```
Name                        Status     Class
----                        ------     -----
Realtek Audio              Disabled   Media
NVIDIA Virtual Audio Dev   Disabled   AudioEndpoint
```

---

### **Pro Tip for Nerd Points**
You can re-enable disabled devices using:

```powershell
Get-PnpDevice -FriendlyName "Your Device Name" | Enable-PnpDevice -Confirm:$false
```

## âš–ï¸ Azure vs. GCP: Service Comparison

### **Compute Services**

- **Azure**: îˆƒOffers a wide range of virtual machines (VMs) with various configurations, including specialized options for high-performance computing.îˆ„îˆ†

- **GCP**: îˆƒProvides flexible VM options with custom machine types, allowing you to tailor resources to specific workloads.îˆ„îˆ†

### **Storage Solutions**

- **Azure**: îˆƒFeatures services like Azure Blob Storage and Azure Files, suitable for unstructured data and file shares.îˆ„îˆ†

- **GCP**: îˆƒOffers Google Cloud Storage with multiple classes (Standard, Nearline, Coldline, Archive) for different access needs.îˆ„îˆ†

### **Networking**

- **Azure**: îˆƒUtilizes Azure Virtual Network for networking needs, with integrated load balancers and VPN gateways.îˆ„îˆ†

- **GCP**: îˆƒEmploys Virtual Private Cloud (VPC) networks, providing global load balancing and Cloud Interconnect options.îˆ„îˆ†

### **AI & Machine Learning**

- **Azure**: îˆƒProvides Azure Machine Learning for building, training, and deploying models, along with Cognitive Services for pre-built AI capabilities.îˆ„îˆ†

- **GCP**: îˆƒOffers Vertex AI for end-to-end machine learning workflows and access to Google's advanced AI models.îˆ„îˆ†

---

## ğŸ’° Pricing Comparison

### **Compute Costs**

- **Azure**: îˆƒPricing varies by VM type and region. Azure offers discounts through Reserved Instances and the Azure Hybrid Benefit for Windows Server licenses.îˆ„îˆ†

## ğŸ”Œ **Snappy Driver Installer (SDI) Question**

### Your Setup:
- SDI is **not Windows Store-approved** but it's a reputable **power-user tool**.
- You **disabled Driver Signature Enforcement**, so itâ€™ll try install unsigned drivers.

### âš ï¸ **Should you use it?**
- **YES for missing or critical device drivers** like audio or chipset.
- **NO for obscure, outdated, or unnecessary devices** â€” especially if Windows is already working fine without them.

ğŸ‘‰ Best practice: **Scan with SDI, but selectively install only the drivers labeled:**
- âœ… "Signed"
- âœ… "Recommended"
- âš ï¸ Avoid "Old", "Beta", or "Unsigned" unless absolutely necessary

### ğŸ§¾ **That â€œ4096 MB Totalâ€ Display Bug**

#### ğŸ›  Method 1: Use Resource Monitor


   ```
   HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Services\disk
   ```


#### ğŸ§  Here's how:

1. Right-click `disk` â†’ **New â†’ Key**

4. Name it: `0`

- "The port with index `0`? Treat it like itâ€™s internal storage."

## ğŸ“ First, Whatâ€™s the Difference Between `.yml` and `.yaml`?

**Literally none.** Theyâ€™re just two file extensions for the **same file type: YAML (YAML Ainâ€™t Markup Language)**.

- `.yaml` = full, official extension
- `.yml` = shorter, legacy-friendly (especially for older Windows and Docker tools)

ğŸ’¡ **Docker doesnâ€™t care** which you use â€” it looks for either:
```
docker-compose.yml
docker-compose.yaml
```
So stick with **`.yml`** like you're already doing â€” itâ€™s cleaner and Dockerâ€™s default.

---

## ğŸ³ What Does `docker-compose.yml` Actually Do?

That file defines **what Docker should run** when you do:
```bash
docker-compose up
```

Itâ€™s like a recipe. It tells Docker:
- What image to use
- What port to open
- Where to mount folders (if needed)
- What the container should be called

---

### ğŸ§  Here's Why You Need to Update It

Your current script ends with:
```bat
docker-compose up -d
```

So that command reads the `docker-compose.yml` in `C:\OpenWebUI`.  
If that file still maps to **port 3000**, then OpenWebUI will still launch on 3000 â€” even though your script says to open 8080.

---

### âœ… TL;DR: The `.bat` file **calls** the `.yml` file

They work together like this:

| File | Role |
|------|------|
| `Launch_Docker_WSL.bat` | Starts Docker, WSL, and OpenWebUI |
| `docker-compose.yml` | Tells Docker *what* OpenWebUI is and *what port to open* |

So yes â€” your `.bat` script is like the **remote**, but the `.yml` is the **TV setup**.

---

user
What command fetches that script?? from where?
ChatGPT
The `docker-compose.yml` script is **not fetched by a command from the internet** â€” it's **executed locally** by Docker using this command in your `.bat` launcher or terminal:

```bash
docker compose up -d
```

### ğŸ” What This Command Does:
- Looks for a file named `docker-compose.yml` in the **current directory**
- Reads the service definitions (like OpenWebUI)
- Pulls the Docker image (`ghcr.io/open-webui/open-webui:main`) from the GitHub Container Registry (GHCR) if itâ€™s not already on your system
- Starts the container using your settings (port 8080, volume, etc.)

> So the **command doesn't fetch the YAML** â€” it **uses it** as an instruction set.

### ğŸ“ Location
Because your `.bat` file does:
```bat
cd /d C:\OpenWebUI
```

Docker knows to look for the `docker-compose.yml` **inside that folder.**

Let me know if you want me to mark that up more clearly in the canvas too ğŸ‘Š

user
Well, now that you bring in Google Drive, can we not then create... Why's it won't work, eh? Maybe it would. Could we maybe create a... a paging file on Google Drive? Would that work?
ChatGPT
Alright Rob â€” this is one of those *"maybe but mostly no"* kind of tech myths. Let's break it down:

---

### âš ï¸ Can We Use Google Drive for a Paging File?

**Short answer:** *Not directly.*

**Long answer:**

#### âŒ You **cannot** set Google Drive itself (the cloud space) as a paging file location.
Windows requires:
- **NTFS file system**
- **Fixed/internal drive** (not seen as removable)
- **Fast random access I/O**

Google Drive is a **sync engine**, not a mounted drive in the OS-level block device sense. Any paging attempt would be:
- **Incredibly slow** (latency from cloud fetch/sync)
- **Unstable** (paging files constantly update = sync storm)
- **Risky** (system crashes if network drops)

---

### âœ… However... There's a Hacky *Hybrid* That **Can Work**:

#### ğŸ‘‰ **Use Google Drive *Sync Folder* (Drive for Desktop)**

If you install Google Drive for Desktop in **Mirror Mode**:
- You can create a `.vhdx` inside a synced folder (e.g. `C:\Users\Rob\Google Drive\MeneSwap\CloudPage.vhdx`)
- Mount that `.vhdx` at boot (via Task Scheduler or `.ps1`)
- Assign it as a paging drive **locally**

ğŸ” **It stays local**, but Google Drive syncs it **in the background**, offering cloud redundancy after shutdown or reboot.

---

### âš ï¸ Huge Caveat

Because paging writes are **non-stop and massive**:
- Google Drive will constantly try to sync the `.vhdx` file
- It'll eat CPU + bandwidth
- Youâ€™ll get **sync errors or corruption** over time

---

### ğŸ§  Best Practice?

- ğŸ§  Use **`F:\AI_RAM`** or **local fixed disk** for paging  
- âœ… Use Google Drive to **sync compressed backups** of your `.vhdx`, *not active ones*  
- ğŸ’¡ Use a **PowerShell script** to:
  - Mount `CloudSwap.vhdx` if local only
  - Avoid syncing while in use
  - Sync only on shutdown/logoff (optional)

---

### âœ… Want me to:

- Generate a script to create a `.vhdx` in your Google Drive folder?
- Auto-mount it (but **not** assign it as a paging file)?
- Or log this in your VHDX canvas with clear *â€œDo Not Use As Pagingâ€* flags?

Let me know, Boet!

Quick quizzy. How do I **disable** super secret admin** in windows 10?

ChatGPT

Lekker one, boet. That "**Super Secret Administrator**" account in Windows 10 is actually just the hidden **built-in Administrator** account â€” itâ€™s more powerful than your regular admin because it bypasses UAC (User Account Control) entirely.

Hereâ€™s how you **disable** it:

---

### **Step-by-step (Command Prompt):**

1. **Open Command Prompt as Administrator**:
   - Hit `Start`, type `cmd`
   - Right-click **Command Prompt** > Select **Run as administrator**

2. **Run this command**:
   ```
   net user administrator /active:no
   ```

3. You should see:  
   `The command completed successfully.`

4. Done. The built-in admin is now disabled and wonâ€™t show up at login.

---

### **Double-check (Optional)**:
If you wanna confirm, run:
```
net user administrator
```
It should say `Account active: No`

---

Need to **re-enable** it later? Just run:
```
net user administrator /active:yes
```

Shout if you need it nuked via PowerShell or policy instead.  
Want me to build a desktop shortcut or script that toggles it?

How do I activate a paging file on C drive in Windows 10?

ChatGPT

Shot Rob! Hereâ€™s how to **activate (or adjust) the paging file on the C: drive** in Windows 10 â€” step-by-step, nice and clean:

---

### **How to Enable or Change Paging File on C: Drive**

1. **Open System Properties**:
   - Press `Windows + R`, type:  
     ```
     sysdm.cpl
     ```
     Hit **Enter**

2. Go to **Advanced** tab  
   - Under â€œPerformanceâ€, click **Settings**

3. In Performance Options:
   - Go to the **Advanced** tab again
   - Under â€œVirtual memoryâ€, click **Changeâ€¦**

4. **Uncheck**:  
   `Automatically manage paging file size for all drives`

5. Select **C: drive** in the list

6. Choose:
   - **System managed size** (Windows handles it)  
   **OR**  
   - **Custom size**:
     - Initial size (MB): ~**1.5x your RAM**  
     - Maximum size (MB): ~**3x your RAM**

   *(e.g. for 8GB RAM = 8192MB â†’ 12288 / 24576)*

7. Click **Set**, then click **OK**

8. Reboot your PC to apply changes.

Nice one, Boet. For **4GB of RAM**, the classic Microsoft recommendation for paging file size is:

---

### **Paging File Size Calculation**  
- **Minimum (Initial size)**: 1.5 Ã— RAM  
- **Maximum size**: 3 Ã— RAM

---

### **For 4GB RAM (4096MB)**:
- **Initial size**:  
  `4096 Ã— 1.5 = 6144 MB`  
- **Maximum size**:  
  `4096 Ã— 3 = 12288 MB`

---

### **Recommended Pagefile Settings for 4GB RAM:**
- **Initial size**: `6144 MB`  
- **Maximum size**: `12288 MB`

---

If you want a tighter setup (less disk usage), you *can* go a bit lower, but these values are safe and standard â€” especially if you're doing multitasking or heavier stuff like AI or media.
